The last enemy makes a final stand or a final attempt to flee. The scout, if alive, draws the shortsword — a desperation weapon at this point, a longbow archer in melee range.

> **DEFENSE ROLL:** Roll 1d20 + DEX modifier | DC 14
> **SUCCESS:** Deflected. The last threat is ended.
> **FAILURE:** 1d6+3 damage. A parting gift.

---

### AFTER THE FIGHT

The observation post is neutralized. The lean-to is wreckage. The fire pit is scattered. The lake is perfectly still and the pre-dawn light makes the water look like hammered steel and for a moment, standing over the aftermath with blood on your hands and the adrenaline fading, you see what Vogler looks like from the perspective of the people who have been watching it and planning its destruction. It is small. It is beautiful. It is completely, terrifyingly vulnerable.

**IF THE HORN WAS NOT BLOWN:** The bridge garrison does not know you are coming. Significant tactical advantage in Scene 70. +2 SWORD.
**IF THE HORN WAS BLOWN:** The bridge will be reinforced. +2 soldiers at Scene 70. The approach will be harder. +1 SWORD (you tried).

> **OBSERVATION POST SUPPLY TENT:** No moral penalty — enemy military supplies. Stash Loot Roll:
> 1–5: Empty ration cans and a cold fire. They were living light.
> 6–10: Supplies (rations, Dragon Army signal flags, 1d4 stl).
> 11–14: 2d6 stl + mundane item (spyglass, maps of the lake shore, patrol schedules).
> 15–17: 3d6 stl + useful item (Potion of Healing, antitoxin, military-grade rope).
> 18–19: 4d6 stl + quality item (Dragon Army attack route map — Caeron will give you +1 to ALL Vogler defence checks for this intel).
> 20: 5d6 stl + RARE ITEM ROLL.

> **RARE ITEM ROLL:** Roll 1d20.
> 1–19: Quality mundane item (the scout's longbow — masterwork, +1 damage, not magical).
> 20: MAGIC ITEM — +1 Studded Leather. Light, supple, elvish make. The scout took it from someone who will not need it anymore. AC 13+DEX.

> **SEARCH THE SCOUT'S PERSONAL EFFECTS (Optional — moral choice):**
> The scout was a person. He had a bedroll, a journal, a locket.
>
> **A) Search.** (-1 ROSE. 1d4 stl + 2 days rations + the journal reveals Dragon Army troop numbers — valuable intel.)
> **B) Leave it. He was the enemy but he was a person.** (+1 ROSE. No loot.)

> **THE WAR DOG** (if calmed via Animal Handling):
> The dog is sitting where you told it to sit. It is a large, grey, short-haired hound with a torn ear and the expression of a dog that has been told to do many things by many people and is waiting to find out what you want. It does not have a name. Or if it does, the name is in a language it no longer obeys.
>
> **A) Take the dog.** (+1 ROSE. The dog follows the column. No combat benefit, but it is a good dog. It sleeps by your feet in Vogler. The villagers' children name it.)
> **B) Let it go.** (The dog watches you leave. It does not follow. It sits by the lake and waits for someone who is not coming back.)

> **AFTER THE FIGHT — UPDATE YOUR SHEET**
> Record current HP. Mark any used: Second Wind, Action Surge, spell slots,
> Lay on Hands pool, bandages, potions consumed.
> Update gold if loot was found. Record any items gained.
> Note if the horn was blown (affects Scene 70 bridge fight).
> Note if the dog was taken (flavour in Vogler).
> **Short rest available:** Recover Action Surge, regain 1 Second Wind use. HP: spend Hit Dice.

> **XP:** 15 XP per soldier. 20 XP for the scout. 10 XP for the dog (if calmed, not killed — +5 bonus). Bonus 15 XP if horn was prevented. Bonus 10 XP if stealth succeeded.

> **A) The post is silent. The bridge is ahead. Ride.** --> Scene 69B

---


## SCENE 69B — THE LAKE AT DAWN

*South Road — Day 3, Final Approach*

The lake appears first as light — the reflection of the sunrise off water, a brightness on the southern horizon that is different from sky-brightness, flatter and wider and the colour of beaten silver. Then the water. Then the village.

Vogler in the early morning is beautiful. That is the thing no one warned you about — that the place you are riding to defend is genuinely, quietly, achingly beautiful. Stone buildings along the waterfront. Boats at the pier. Smoke from morning cooking fires. A stand of aspens at the east end that have gone gold with autumn and are catching the sunrise in a way that looks like the trees are on fire in the good way.

Aldric rides beside you. He sees the lake and for a moment his face does something it almost never does: it opens. Then it reassembles. But you saw it.

**IF MIRA IS WITH YOU:** She stops her horse. 'My mother's village looked like this.' Very quiet. Then she rides on.

**IF TARIEL IS WITH YOU:** Her notebook stays closed. This is not an observation she is willing to reduce to writing.

**IF LYSANDER IS WITH YOU:** He is standing at the ridgeline, separate from the column, looking at the lake. He looks exactly like a Knight of the Sword on the morning of a battle, which is what he is whether the paperwork agrees or not.

> **A) Stop. Look. Remember this.** (+1 ROSE) --> Scene 70
>
> **B) 'It is worth saving.'** (+1 ROSE, +1 SWORD) --> Scene 70
>
> **C) Ride on. The beauty does not change the numbers.** (+1 SWORD, +1 CROWN) --> Scene 70
>
> **D) (If polyamory accepted) The morning with both of them.** (ROMANCE) --> Scene DATE_POLY_MORN

---


## SCENE 70 — THE BRIDGE

*Vogler Bridge — Late Afternoon, Day 3*

The bridge is old stone — Solamnic work, pre-Cataclysm, built for carts and cavalry and the kind of permanence that empires use to say *this is ours*. It spans the river where it feeds into Lake Akh-Tiel, sixty feet of dressed granite with moss on the pilings and, at the far end, four soldiers in dark armour who are not Solamnic.

They are setting up a checkpoint. Wooden barricade. Two swords, a polearm, and the sergeant — you can tell the sergeant because he carries himself the way Caeron carries himself, like someone whose posture is load-bearing. The Dragon Army standard hangs from a pole jammed between the bridge stones: the five-headed dragon, the colours bleached by lake wind. They have been here long enough to be bored. Bored soldiers are either less dangerous or more. You cannot tell yet.

**IF THE HORN WAS BLOWN AT 70A:** Two additional soldiers flank the barricade. The sergeant knew you were coming. His posture is not bored. It is ready. Six enemies, not four.

Caeron halts the column with a fist. He looks at you. Not at Aldric, not at Sera, not at the veterans. At you. 'Your call, Squire. We can try to parley. Or we take the bridge.'

Aldric's hand is on his sword. He has not drawn it. He is waiting for you. The weight of that — Aldric Voss, who has spent eight months competing with you, waiting for your decision — is a specific kind of trust that the Oath describes but cannot teach.

**IF SERA IS PRESENT:** She has already counted them. 'Four. Sergeant in the rear. Polearm is the reach problem. The barricade is oak — it will hold a charge but not a flank.' She pauses. 'If we talk first and it fails, we lose surprise. If we hit first, we keep it. Your call.'

**IF THESSALY IS PRESENT:** She is writing. She writes during everything. But her hand is shaking. 'Whatever you do,' she says, not looking up, 'do it before I finish this sentence, because I am documenting the last moment before.'

On the far side of the bridge, beyond the soldiers, you can see Vogler. The rooftops. The fishing boats. A woman hanging laundry who has stopped to look at the bridge. She is holding a sheet that catches the wind. She cannot see the soldiers from her angle. She does not know yet.

> **ROLL REQUIRED:** Perception (+2) | WIS | DC 14
>
> **SUCCESS (roll 14+):** The sergeant is watching the road, not the column. You see the gap in the barricade where the oak boards do not meet. If you move now, they will not be ready. The party gains surprise.
>
> **FAILURE (roll 1-13):** No surprise — the sergeant spots you as you approach. Initiative rolled normally.

> **COMBAT ENCOUNTER — THE BRIDGE CHECKPOINT**
>
> **Pen & Paper:** Use stat blocks below. Run at your table, then skip to AFTER THE BRIDGE.
> **Narrative Mode:** Run the fight. Roll attacks against AC. Roll defence against DC 14 each enemy round.
>
> **ENEMIES:**
> • Human Soldier x4 (x6 if horn blown): AC 14, HP 16 each. Longsword +4 (1d8+2). Formation: +1 AC when adjacent to another soldier.
> • Sergeant (one of the four): AC 15, HP 22. Longsword +5 (1d10+3). Bonus action Rally: one ally rerolls a failed attack. Last Stand: when reduced below half HP, advantage on attacks for 1 round.
>
> **TERRAIN:** The bridge is 10 feet wide. No flanking — the water is 20 feet below. The barricade provides half cover (+2 AC) to enemies behind it. You can vault the barricade with Athletics DC 12.
>
> **COMPANION COMBAT ACTIONS:**
> **Aldric:** A) Flank (+1 attack) | B) Shield Wall (+1 AC) | C) Bridge Rush — charges the barricade (Athletics DC 12, clears a 5ft gap, takes 1d4 from polearm)
> **Sera:** A) Aimed Shot (1d6+3 at sergeant) | B) Mark Target (+2 next attack) | C) Covering Fire (+1 AC to you)
> **Mira:** A) Heal 1d6+2 (1/scene) | B) Bandage (stabilize 1 HP) | C) Herbal Boost (+1 save)
> **Vael:** A) Flanking Shot (1d6 dmg) | B) Honest Assessment (reveal all HP) | C) Cover Fire (+1 AC)
> **Lysander:** A) Veteran Strike (1d10+3) | B) Combat Instruction (+2 attack) | C) Guard (takes next hit for you)
>
> **ABILITY / ITEM USE (choose one, optional):**
> • (Paladin) Divine Smite: Bonus action after hit. 2d8 radiant (+1d8 vs Fiends/Undead). Once per turn. 1 spell slot (or free 1/long rest at level 2+).
> • (Paladin) Lay on Hands: Bonus action. Heal from pool.
> • (Paladin) Bless: +1d4 attacks/saves, 3 targets, Concentration, entire combat. 1 spell slot.
> • (Fighter) Action Surge: Additional Action (except Magic). 1/short or long rest.
> • (Fighter) Second Wind: 1d10+level HP. Bonus action. 2 uses/long rest, regain 1/short rest.
> • (Any) Potion of Healing: 2d4+2 HP. Bonus action.

### AFTER THE BRIDGE

The last soldier falls. The bridge is yours. Your arms are shaking — not from fear, from the specific exhaustion that comes after a fight on a narrow bridge where every step mattered and every missed parry would have sent you into twenty feet of cold water.

Aldric is breathing hard beside you. There is blood on his tabard — not his. He looks at you and his face does the thing where it reassembles, but slower this time, as if the parts that make up the mask are tired of pretending.

**IF MIRA IS PRESENT:** She is already moving — checking the fallen, not for enemies, for casualties. 'Ours first,' she says. Then she checks the Dragon Army soldiers too, because that is who she is.

**IF VAEL IS PRESENT:** She kicks the Dragon Army standard into the river. Watches it float south. 'They will know we are here now,' she says. Then, quieter: 'Good.'

Then you look up. On the far side, at the edge of the village, people are standing in the street. The woman with the laundry has dropped the sheet. A child is pointing. An old man is leaning in a doorway with his hand over his mouth. They watched the whole thing. They saw Solamnic tabards fight for the bridge into their village and they are standing in the street not because they are brave but because hope requires witnesses.

> **AFTER THE FIGHT — UPDATE YOUR SHEET**
> Record current HP. Mark any used: Second Wind, Action Surge, spell slots,
> Lay on Hands pool, bandages, potions consumed.
> **Short rest available:** Recover Action Surge, regain 1 Second Wind use.

> **A) Sheathe your sword. Walk across the bridge. They are waiting.** (+1 ROSE) --> Scene 70B
>
> **B) Aldric beside you, tabard bloodied, walk into Vogler like what you are: knights.** (+1 SWORD) --> Scene 70B
>
> **C) Let Caeron lead. He has earned the right to enter first.** (+1 CROWN) --> Scene 70B

---


## SCENE 70B — THE PEOPLE OF VOGLER

*Vogler — Bridge Square, Late Afternoon*

They are waiting on the other side. Not a crowd — Vogler is too small and too practical for crowds — but a gathering, the kind that forms when people have been watching from windows and walls and have decided, individually, to come down. A woman with flour on her hands. A fisherman still carrying his net, knuckles white on the handle. Three children who have been told to stay inside and have not. An old man leaning on a younger man, both watching you cross the bridge with the expression of people evaluating whether hope is a reasonable investment.

You see them all at once and it hits you like a shield blow: these are eighty-four people and you are what stands between them and the Dragon Army.

The woman with flour says: 'How many of you are there?' You tell her. She looks at Aldric, at Caeron, at the column behind you. She counts. The number is not what she hoped. She nods anyway. 'Come in. There is food.'

The fisherman steps forward. His name is Torren. He has the hands of a man who has hauled nets in every weather for thirty years and the eyes of a man who knows what two thousand soldiers will do to a village of eighty-four. 'The Dragon Army — how long?'

**IF ALDRIC IS IN YOUR PARTY:** Aldric has stopped walking. One of the children — a boy, maybe six, missing a front tooth — has walked up to him and is staring at his tabard with the expression of absolute certainty that knights are invincible. Aldric kneels. 'What is your name?' The boy says *Tam*. Aldric puts his hand on Tam's shoulder, and you see the weight of it settle into him — the understanding that this is not a drill, not a formation exercise, not a score on a sheet. This is a boy named Tam who believes knights do not lose. Aldric stands. His jaw is set in the way that means he has decided something, and the thing he has decided is that Tam is going to be right.

**IF MIRA IS IN YOUR PARTY:** Mira is already moving. A woman at the back of the gathering is holding a baby that is coughing — the wet, rattling cough that means something worse than a cold. Mira reaches her before anyone else notices. Her hands are on the child's chest, listening through touch, already pulling dried yarrow from her belt pouch. 'How long has she been coughing?' The mother's face does the thing faces do when someone competent arrives: it breaks, just for a moment, with relief. Mira looks back at you across the square, and what her look says is: *This village has no healer. They have been managing with hope and prayer and whatever the fisherman's wife remembers about poultices.* She turns back to the baby. She is not going to leave this square until that cough is addressed.

**IF SERA IS IN YOUR PARTY:** Sera's eyes are moving. Not looking at the people — looking at the walls, the rooflines, the approaches from the south. You have seen her do this at every camp, every waystation, every clearing. She is reading the ground the way a scholar reads a page. After thirty seconds she says, very quietly, so only you hear: 'The bridge is the only approach for heavy infantry. The lakeshore is soft ground — bad footing for a charge. The watchtower ridge gives sightline for half a mile south.' She pauses. 'This is defensible. Not easily. But defensibly.' From Sera, this is optimism.

**IF THESSALY IS IN YOUR PARTY:** Thessaly has her notebook open. She is not writing strategy or military assessment. She is writing *names*. The flour woman is Bess. The fisherman is Torren. The old man is Elder Isak's brother, Pettar, who cannot walk without help but who came to the bridge anyway because he wanted to see knights arrive with his own eyes. The children are Tam, Elsa, and the youngest whose name no one has told her yet because the youngest is hiding behind Bess's skirt. Thessaly writes them all down. When you catch her eye she says: 'Someone should know who lived here. Before.'

**IF CAERON IS IN YOUR PARTY:** Caeron is doing the thing he does — the thing you have watched him do at every briefing and every camp and every crossroads since Brightholm. He is being composed. He is shaking Torren's hand with exactly the right firmness. He is telling Bess that the food would be welcome. He is nodding at the right moments and saying the right words. But you have spent enough weeks with Lord Caeron Ashvale to see the fracture line. It is in his eyes, in the moment between one sentence and the next. He looked at Vogler and did the arithmetic and the arithmetic does not work and he is going to do it anyway.

**IF YOU SAVED THE REFUGEES IN ACT TWO:** Someone in the gathering recognises the tabard. A woman pushes forward — you have seen her face before, on the south road, streaked with dust. 'The knights from the south road. They helped my family.' A murmur moves through the group. Torren looks at you differently. The investment in hope becomes slightly more reasonable.

**IF LINNA WAS SAVED (Scene 44C):** A small voice: '*That is them.*' Linna is here. She is thin and her eyes are too old for her face but she is alive and standing and pointing at you with the absolute authority of a child who has decided you are a hero. Her family surrounds her — mother, uncle, a grandmother who takes your hand in both of hers and says nothing because nothing is sufficient. Mira, if she is present, puts her hand on Linna's head and checks her fever by touch. The fever is gone. 'You did good work,' Mira says, and she means the people who kept Linna alive on the road, but she is looking at you.

**IF YOU TURNED THE REFUGEES BACK:** No one recognises you. The tabard is enough. You carry the weight of that, and standing among the people of Vogler, the weight is heavier than it was on the road.

> **A) 'Two days. Maybe less. We will help you prepare.'** (+1 CROWN, +1 ROSE) --> Scene 70C
>
> **B) 'We need to see your defences. Now.' Caeron is already moving.** (+1 CROWN) --> Scene 70C
>
> **C) Accept the food first. These people need to feel useful.** (+2 ROSE) --> Scene 70C

---


## SCENE 70C — THE ELDER OF VOGLER

*Vogler — Elder Isak's House, Evening*

The elder's name is Isak. His house is the largest in the village, which means it has two rooms and a table long enough for a map. The lamp on the table is whale oil — the good kind, the kind that burns clean. He has been the elder of Vogler for twenty-three years and his handshake has the particular strength of a man who has been making difficult decisions for longer than you have been alive. His eyes are clear. He has not been sleeping and he is not going to apologise for it.

He unrolls his own map — hand-drawn, accurate, annotated in a steady hand that has not shaken in twenty-three years and is not going to start now. The bridge you crossed. The lake shore with its depth markings. The grain stores, marked with quantities and dates. The fishing fleet — four boats, one leaking. The old Solamnic watchtower on the eastern ridge, abandoned since the Cataclysm but still standing because Solamnic engineers built things to last, even when the world they built them for did not.

'We have eighty-four souls,' he says. 'Eleven who can fight. The rest are families, children, elders. I will not send them away because there is nowhere to send them that is safer than here with you.' He says it without self-pity. He says it the way you state a tactical fact.

He looks at Caeron. 'What do you need from us?'

Caeron studies the map. The silence stretches. You have seen Caeron evaluate terrain a dozen times and he has never taken this long. Then you understand: it is not the terrain that is giving him trouble. It is the numbers. Eighty-four souls. Two thousand soldiers. The arithmetic does not work. He knows it. Isak knows it. Everyone at this table knows it.

'Everything,' Caeron says. And begins.

He starts with the bridge — the chokepoint, the only advantage they have. Four men abreast, maximum. Funnel them. Then the watchtower — sightlines, signal fires, early warning. Then the grain stores — if the siege lasts more than three days, rationing begins immediately. Then the boats — evacuation route for non-combatants if the line breaks. He does not say *when* the line breaks. He says *if*. Isak notices. You notice that Isak notices.

**IF MIRA IS IN YOUR PARTY:** Mira has been standing by the wall, listening. When Caeron says *eighty-four souls, eleven who can fight*, her eyes close. Just for a moment. You remember Scene 68 — *I know the arithmetic does not work.* This is the arithmetic made visible. Eighty-four people with names, eleven who have held a weapon in the last decade, and a baby in the square who was coughing. When she opens her eyes she steps forward to the map and puts her finger on the grain store. 'This building. I need it. Not for grain — for a field hospital. If the fighting starts I will need clean water, flat surfaces, and light. The grain can go in the boats.' Isak looks at her. He looks at the map. He moves the grain allocation to the boats without argument. Mira nods. She is already planning where to put the wounded. She is already planning for wounds that have not happened yet to people she has not met. This is what she does. This is what she has always done.

**IF ALDRIC IS IN YOUR PARTY:** Aldric is studying the bridge approach on the map. His finger traces the sightlines from the watchtower. 'If we station archers on the ridge, the bridge becomes a kill zone for sixty yards.' He pauses. 'The problem is the lakeshore. Soft ground, but not impassable. If they send a flanking force through the shallows at dawn...' Caeron looks at him. Something passes between them — the recognition that Aldric has been paying attention this entire journey, that the competitive squire from Brightholm has become someone who reads terrain like a knight. 'Aldric,' Caeron says. 'You have the watchtower.' It is not a question. It is the first time Caeron has given Aldric a command assignment instead of a training assignment. Aldric straightens. 'Yes, my lord.'

**IF SERA IS IN YOUR PARTY:** Sera is at the window, looking south. She has been silent since entering the house. When Caeron finishes his assessment she says: 'They will send scouts first. A day ahead of the main force. Three or four, moving through the tree line east of the road. If we take them quietly, the main force loses their sightline on the village for six to eight hours. That is enough time to finish the bridge barricade.' Everyone looks at her. She does not elaborate. She does not need to. 'I will need one companion and no torches,' she adds.

**IF THESSALY IS IN YOUR PARTY:** She is already taking notes. Not strategy — Caeron has that. She is writing the record. Isak's map. The grain quantities. The names of the eleven who can fight. The names of the seventy-three who cannot. She is building a document because someone should, and because the people of Vogler deserve to be more than a footnote in whatever history survives. At one point Isak watches her write and says, quietly: 'You are the journalist.' 'Yes.' 'Will anyone read it?' Thessaly looks up from her notebook. 'Yes,' she says. And she means it like a promise.

**IF SERATH IS IN YOUR PARTY:** Serath stands in the corner, arms folded, face unreadable. They know Dragon Army tactics better than anyone at this table. When Caeron finishes, Serath says: 'They will not negotiate. The standing order for lakeside settlements is pacification, not occupation. They will burn the boats first to prevent evacuation, then push the bridge.' A silence. 'I know this because I helped write that doctrine.' Another silence. Isak looks at Serath the way you look at a weapon you are not sure you trust. Serath meets his gaze. 'I am here. That should tell you something.'

> **A) Help Caeron plan the defence. The watchtower. The bridge. The grain stores.** (+1 CROWN, +1 SWORD) --> Scene 71B
>
> **B) Talk to the villagers. Learn their names. They should know who is fighting for them.** (+2 ROSE) --> Scene 71B
>
> **C) Walk the perimeter with Aldric. See the ground with your own eyes.** (+1 SWORD) --> Scene 71B
>
> **D) The village has a general store. Last chance to prepare before whatever comes next.** --> Scene 70C_SHOP
>
> **E) (If Sera is in your party) Sera catches your eye. 'The scouts. Tonight. Before they can signal.' She needs one companion and no torches.** (+1 SWORD) --> Scene 70C_SCOUTS

---


## SCENE 70C_SHOP — THE VOGLER GENERAL STORE

*Vogler — Market Square, Evening*

The store is a stone building with a wooden sign that reads MAREN'S in paint that was once red. Inside, it smells of grain, dried fish, and lamp oil. Maren is a woman in her fifties who runs the store, the village accounts, and — from the look of the ledgers stacked behind the counter — most of Vogler's civil administration. She watches you come in with the expression of someone who has been expecting Solamnic soldiers and has already decided what she thinks about it.

'You will need supplies,' she says. It is not a question. She has been listening to Caeron's plans. She knows what is coming better than you do.

> **SHOP — VOGLER GENERAL STORE**
> Browse the wares. Prices in steel pieces (stl). Check your character sheet for current gold.
>
> | Item | Price | Notes |
> |------|-------|-------|
> | Bandage (1 use) | 1 stl | Stabilize at 1 HP |
> | Potion of Healing | 5 stl | 2d4+2 HP, bonus action in combat |
> | Rations (3 days) | 1 stl | Travel supplies |
> | Antitoxin | 3 stl | Advantage on poison saves for 1 hour |
> | Whetstone | 1 stl | +1 damage on next attack (single use) |
> | Rope (50 ft) | 1 stl | Climbing, binding |
> | Fishing net | 1 stl | Restrain, trap (improvised) |
> | Lamp oil (3 flasks) | 1 stl | Fire, light, area denial |
>
> **IF MERCHANT DORREN WAS SAVED (Scene 28_CARAVAN):**
> Dorren arrived ahead of you. His stock supplements Maren's. All prices reduced by 10% (round down). Additional items available:
> | Item | Price | Notes |
> |------|-------|-------|
> | Greater Potion of Healing | 13 stl | 4d4+4 HP (normally 15 stl) |
> | Caltrops (bag) | 2 stl | 10ft area, DC 12 DEX or 1 piercing + speed 0 |
> | Signal whistle | 1 stl | Audible 300ft. Tactical coordination. |
>
> **RARE STOCK ROLL (once per visit):**
> Roll 1d20. Maren reaches under the counter.
> 1–15: Standard stock only. 'This is a fishing village. We sell what we have.'
> 16–18: Uncommon — Greater Potion of Healing available (15 stl, 4d4+4 HP). 'My grandmother made these. The recipe works.'
> 19: Uncommon — Masterwork fishing spear available (as spear, +1 damage, not magical, 15 stl). 'It was meant for sturgeon. It will work for other things.'
> 20: RARE — +1 Shield available (50 stl). Old. Solamnic rose barely visible under the tarnish. It was in the watchtower. Maren does not know its history. You can feel it.
>
> *Record purchases on your character sheet. Deduct steel spent.*

Maren counts your steel with practiced fingers. 'Defend my village,' she says, and it is not a request. It is the price of doing business.

> **A) Done. Return to Elder Isak's planning.** --> Scene 70C

---


## SCENE 70C_SCOUTS — THE SCOUT HUNT

*Vogler — Eastern Tree Line, Night*

Sera leads. You follow. There are no torches — she was specific about this, and she was right. The moonlight through the canopy is enough for her, and after ten minutes of following the shadow ahead of you, it becomes enough for you too. Your eyes adjust. The forest resolves into shapes: trunk, branch, undergrowth, the path Sera is threading through all of it with the silent certainty of a woman who has spent more of her life in forests than in buildings.

She stops. Her hand comes up — the scout's signal for *halt, silence, enemy ahead*. You freeze. Through the trees, fifty yards downslope toward the lake shore: firelight. Low and disciplined, the kind of fire that soldiers build — visible only from one angle, shielded on three sides by stacked stones. Dragon Army field doctrine. Serath was right about the advance scouts.

Sera kneels beside you. Her mouth is at your ear. Her breath is warm and her voice is so quiet it barely qualifies as sound: 'Three. Two soldiers, one scout with a longbow. The scout is the problem — she is good. If she gets a signal arrow off, the main force knows we are here and the bridge assault becomes harder.'

She draws her bow. 'I take the scout. You take the soldiers. Or—' She looks at you. 'You can try to take them all quietly. Stealth approach, disable the signal fire, and they never know what hit them.'

> **APPROACH — Choose your method:**
>
> **A) Sera's plan. She takes the scout, you take the soldiers. Clean and coordinated.** (+1 SWORD, +1 CROWN)
> Sera gets one free attack on the scout (automatic hit, 1d8+3). Then combat begins with surprise — enemies do not act Round 1.
>
> **B) Full stealth. Approach the camp, disable the signal fire first, then engage.** (+2 SWORD)
> **ROLL REQUIRED:** Stealth (+2) | DEX | DC 15
> **SUCCESS:** You reach the signal fire. One action to scatter it. Then combat with surprise AND no signal possible.
> **FAILURE:** A branch snaps. The scout's head turns. Combat begins — no surprise. The scout will attempt to light the signal arrow in Round 1.
>
> **C) Call out to them. Offer surrender. They are three soldiers, not an army.** (+1 ROSE, +1 CROWN)
> **ROLL REQUIRED:** Persuasion (+2) | CHA | DC 16
> **SUCCESS:** They are tired. They are three scouts watching a lake village and they did not expect Solamnic knights. Two of the soldiers throw down weapons. The scout hesitates — then lowers the bow. +2 CROWN. No combat. Prisoners who can be questioned.
> **FAILURE:** The scout fires the signal arrow. +2 soldiers at the bridge fight (Scene 70). Then combat begins.

> **COMBAT MODE**
> **Pen & Paper:** Use stat blocks below. Run at your table. Skip to AFTER THE FIGHT.
> **Narrative Mode:** Follow rounds below. Roll when prompted.
>
> **ENEMIES:**
> **Dragon Army Scout (1):** AC 15, HP 18, Speed 30 ft. Longbow +6 (1d8+3, 150/600). Shortsword +4 (1d6+2). Observant: advantage on Perception. **Priority action: Fire signal arrow (1 action). If signal arrow is fired, +2 soldiers reinforce the bridge in Scene 70.**
> **Dragon Army Soldier (2):** AC 14 (chain shirt), HP 14, Speed 30 ft. Longsword +4 (1d8+2). Shield bash +4 (1d4+2, target DC 12 STR or pushed 5 ft).
>
> **YOUR STATS:** Per character sheet.
>
> **COMPANION: SERA NIGHTHOLLOW (mandatory)**
> | Action A | Action B | Action C |
> |----------|----------|----------|
> | Aimed Shot (1d8+3 at any target) | Pin Down (target cannot move or signal this round) | Silent Kill (if target is below 5 HP, automatic kill, no noise) |
>
> **ENVIRONMENT:** Forest clearing, 30 ft diameter. Signal fire in centre. Stacked stone windbreak (half cover). Trees (full cover, 5 ft from clearing edge). Darkness: disadvantage on attacks beyond 30 ft unless you have darkvision or the fire is lit.

> **ROUND 1 — SHADOWS AND STEEL**
>
> *The clearing is small. The soldiers are relaxed — they have been here for two days, watching a fishing village, and nothing has happened. They do not expect Solamnic knights in the dark. They should.*
>
> **IF SURPRISE:** Enemies do not act this round. You and Sera act freely.
>
> **YOUR ATTACK:**
> **ROLL REQUIRED:** Attack roll (+5 or per sheet) | DC 14 (Soldier AC) or DC 15 (Scout AC)
>
> **HIT:** Deal weapon damage. In the dark, the blade comes from nowhere. The soldier goes down before he fully turns.
>
> **SERA'S ACTION:** Choose from her action table.
>
> **IF NO SURPRISE — SCOUT ACTS:**
> The scout reaches for the signal arrow. She has one round to fire it.
> **INTERRUPT:** If you or Sera target the scout this round and hit, the signal is prevented.
> **If signal fires:** A burning arrow arcs into the sky over the lake. Somewhere south, someone sees it. +2 soldiers at the bridge. The tactical advantage is gone but the fight continues.

> **ROUND 2 — CLOSE QUARTERS**
>
> *The clearing is chaos now — close, brutal, the kind of fighting that happens when the space is too small for formations and the light is too low for tactics. This is not a spar. This is not a training exercise. This is two soldiers who are going to die in the dark if you are good enough and fast enough and willing to do what Sera brought you here to do.*
>
> **YOUR ATTACK:**
> **ROLL REQUIRED:** Attack roll (+5 or per sheet) | DC 14 (Soldier) or DC 15 (Scout)
>
> **(Paladin) OPTIONAL — DIVINE SMITE:** +2d8 radiant. The divine light in the dark forest is blinding. The remaining enemies are stunned for one round (no actions).
>
> **(Fighter) OPTIONAL — ACTION SURGE:** Second attack. End this.
>
> **ENEMY ATTACKS (surviving enemies):**
> **Soldier:** Longsword. Defense roll DC 14. Hit: 1d8+2 slashing.
> **Scout (if alive):** Longbow at range or shortsword in melee. Defense roll DC 16 (bow) or DC 14 (sword). Hit: 1d8+3 or 1d6+2.

> **ROUND 3 (if needed) — FINISHING**
>
> Any remaining enemies fight or flee. A soldier at half HP may break for the tree line.
> **OPTIONAL PURSUIT:** Athletics DC 13 to catch a fleeing soldier. If they escape, +1 soldier at the bridge.

> **AFTER THE FIGHT**
>
> The clearing is quiet. The signal fire is out — scattered or never lit. Sera moves through the bodies with the professional efficiency of someone who has done this before and does not enjoy it but does not hesitate.
>
> 'Clean,' she says. She means the operation, not the killing. She is not happy about the killing. She is satisfied that the killing was necessary and executed well, which is a different thing, and the difference is important to her even if she would never explain it.
>
> **SIGNAL PREVENTED:** +2 SWORD. The bridge garrison does not get reinforcements. Caeron will not know about this mission unless you tell him. Sera will not tell him. 'Some things do not need to be in the report,' she says. 'This was between us and the trees.'
>
> **SIGNAL FIRED:** +1 SWORD. The bridge fight will be harder — two extra soldiers. Sera's jaw tightens. 'It could have been cleaner. We will compensate.' She means she will compensate. With arrows. At the bridge.
>
> **PRISONERS TAKEN (if surrender accepted):** +2 CROWN. Under questioning, the scouts confirm: the Dragon Army assaults at dawn, two days from now. The main force is split — infantry on the road, draconians through the forest. This intelligence is critical. Caeron will want it immediately.
>
> **IF SERA'S ROMANCE IS ACTIVE:** On the walk back, Sera is silent for a long time. Then: 'I have not worked with a partner in the field in three years. The last one—' She stops. Continues. 'You are better. That is all I will say about it.' From Sera, this is a love letter.
>
> > **SEARCH THE CAMP (Optional):**
> > Enemy scouts travel light but carry intel.
> >
> > **A) Search them.** (No moral penalty — military operation.)
> > **Loot Roll:** Roll 1d20.
> > 1-5: 1d4 stl. Standard military kit.
> > 6-10: 2d4 stl. Scout's coded notebook (Serath can decode if present: +1 CROWN).
> > 11-14: 3d4 stl. Quiver of 20 arrows + a map of Vogler's approaches (share with Caeron: +1 CROWN).
> > 15-17: 4d4 stl. Above + Potion of Healing.
> > 18-19: 5d4 stl. Above + masterwork longbow (1d8+DEX, +1 damage, not magical).
> > 20: 6d4 stl. All above + Dragon Army battle plan for Vogler (share with Caeron: +2 CROWN, advantage on one Vogler defense check).
> >
> > **B) Leave it. The mission is done.** The forest can keep what the forest has.
>
> **AFTER THE FIGHT — UPDATE YOUR SHEET**
> Record current HP. Mark any used: Second Wind, Action Surge, spell slots, Lay on Hands pool, bandages, potions consumed.
> **Short rest available:** Walk back to Vogler. Recover Second Wind, Action Surge.
> **XP:** 15 XP per soldier, 20 XP for scout. Bonus 20 XP if signal prevented. Bonus 15 XP if prisoners taken.

> **A) Return to Vogler. The scouts are dealt with. The bridge will be cleaner for it.** --> Scene 70C

---


## SCENE 71B — THE OATH AT VOGLER

*Vogler — Watchtower Ridge, Dusk*

Caeron finds you at sunset. The sky behind him is the colour of old gold — the lake catching the last light, the watchtower casting a shadow that reaches all the way to the village below. He is carrying something wrapped in oilcloth, and when he unwraps it you see the book you have seen on his desk at every camp since Brightholm: his personal copy of the Measure, the one with the cracked spine and the margin notes he has never let anyone read. He sets it on the watchtower stone with the care of a man placing something sacred on an altar that is not quite the right altar but is the one that exists.

Behind you, the village. Cook fires starting. The sound of Torren's voice, carrying, telling someone to bring the nets inside. The baby is not coughing anymore. Below the ridge, the people of Vogler are making dinner and making plans and making the small movements of ordinary life that have no idea they are about to become extraordinary.

'This is not how it is supposed to be done,' Caeron says. His voice is steady the way a bowstring is steady — under tension, controlled, capable of release. 'The ceremony requires a chapter hall. Witnesses of rank. Candles. Three senior knights. The candidate kneels on stone that has been blessed by a cleric of Kiri-Jolith.' He looks at the watchtower, at the cracked flagstones, at the lake. 'We have a watchtower and a village and the Dragon Army two days south.' He pauses. The sunset paints him. 'I have decided that is enough.'

He opens the book. The pages are soft with use, the margins dense with his small, precise handwriting — years of a man's private argument with the principles he has built his life around. He finds the page without looking. He has it memorised. He reads it anyway because the ceremony requires it, and Lord Caeron Ashvale does not skip steps, not even now, not even here, not even when the world is ending.

'The Oath of the Knights of Solamnia. Sworn before Paladine and Kiri-Jolith and the witness of your companions. *Est Sularus oth Mithas* — My honour is my life.'

The words. You have read them a hundred times. You have debated them with Aldous in a courtyard that feels like another life. You have watched people fail to live up to them and succeed in spite of them and struggle with them and lean on them and curse them and come back to them. Standing on a watchtower above a village full of people whose names you learned hours ago, with the lake turning gold and the smoke on the southern horizon and the people below you who are trusting you with everything they have because they do not have anything else to trust — the words feel different.

They feel like yours.

> **HOW DO YOU TAKE THE OATH?**
>
> **A) Kneeling. The stone is cold and cracked and it is enough. You kneel because the people below you need knights who kneel.** (+2 ROSE) You say the words with your eyes closed. When you open them, Caeron's hand is on your shoulder and his face is doing the thing it almost never does: opening, just for a moment, into something that is not composure but belief.
>
> **B) Standing. Sword drawn, point down. The Oath of the Sword is not taken on your knees.** (+2 SWORD) You say the words facing south — toward the smoke, toward what is coming. Your blade catches the last light. Caeron watches you, and for the first time since Brightholm, he does not correct your form. There is nothing to correct.
>
> **C) Standing beside Caeron, as an equal. 'This is how it is supposed to be done,' you say. 'Before the people we protect.'** (+2 CROWN) Something shifts in Caeron's expression. Not surprise — recognition. He closes the book. He does not need it. He never needed it. He says the words with you, and for the space of those words, the distance between Lord Commander and squire disappears entirely.

**IF ALDRIC IS IN YOUR PARTY:** He swears beside you. The same words. The same sunset. His voice does not shake but his hands do, slightly, and you notice because you have learned to notice, and he knows you notice and does not hide it. When the words are done he looks at you sideways and there is something in his face that was not there at Brightholm — not competition, not admiration, something older and simpler than both. He chose to stand where you are standing. He chose it every day since the south road. This is the moment that choice becomes permanent. He does not say anything. He does not need to.

**IF MIRA IS IN YOUR PARTY:** She watches from the edge of the ridge. She does not kneel. She has no oath to take and she does not need one — she is already living the principle the Oath describes, and has been since before you met her, since the first bandage she tied on a stranger's wound because the wound existed and her hands were capable. When you meet her eyes she nods. Once. It is enough. It is more than a salute. It is a healer acknowledging that the person in front of her has chosen the wound, chosen to stand between the blade and the body behind them, and she will be there when the choosing has consequences. She always is.

**IF SERA IS IN YOUR PARTY:** She is standing at the edge of the ridge, watching the southern tree line. She does not turn for the ceremony. She does not need to. Her presence *is* her oath — the scout who stays, the ranger who could vanish into any forest on Ansalon and has chosen, for reasons she will not articulate and you will not ask about, to stand on this ridge with these people. When the words are done, she glances back. 'Beautiful,' she says, and it takes you a moment to realise she means the sunset, not the ceremony. Then another moment to realise she means both.

**IF THESSALY IS IN YOUR PARTY:** Her pen has stopped. She has been writing everything — every scene, every conversation, every choice you have made since Brightholm — and now, for the first time, the pen is still. She is watching you take the Oath and she is not recording it. Some moments are not for the record. Some moments are for the person witnessing them. When the words are done she picks up the pen and writes one line. You do not ask what it is. Years later, when the chronicle is published, you will find it: *They meant it. I was there. They meant every word.*

**IF VAEL IS IN YOUR PARTY:** She stands at attention. Not the relaxed posture she usually holds — full, formal, the stance of a Crown knight's daughter who grew up watching ceremonies and learned to see the shape of duty before she learned to spell it. Her eyes are bright. She does not cry. She would be furious if you suggested she was close to it. But her chin is up and her shoulders are back and when the words are done she salutes — not you, not Caeron, but the watchtower itself, the old stone, the Solamnic architecture that has survived the Cataclysm and the abandonment and the centuries, and is still standing.

**IF SERATH IS IN YOUR PARTY:** They stand apart. They do not take the Oath. They cannot — they are not Solamnic, they are barely an ally, they are a defector and a spy and a person whose relationship with oaths is complicated in ways that would take a second book to unpack. But they are here. They are standing on a Solamnic watchtower watching Solamnic knights swear an oath they once fought against, and they are not leaving. When your eyes find them, Serath inclines their head. It is the gesture of someone who does not believe in your institution but believes, against their own judgment, in you.

**IF LYSANDER IS IN YOUR PARTY:** He leans against the watchtower wall with his arms crossed. He has no rank, no order, no oath of his own — just a sword and a lifetime of reasons not to trust institutions. He does not kneel. He does not salute. But he is here, and he did not have to be. When the ceremony is done he pushes off the wall and says: 'Well. Now you have said the words.' He looks south, toward the smoke. 'Let us see if the words hold.' It is the closest thing to approval Lysander Graves has ever given the Knights of Solamnia. It is enough.

The sun sets. The lake goes from gold to copper to the deep bruised purple of a Krynn evening. Below you, the village is lit by cook fires and lamp oil. You can hear, faintly, a child laughing — Tam, probably, who believes knights are invincible and whose belief, in this moment, on this ridge, with these words still warm in your mouth, feels less like innocence and more like a commission.

You are a Squire of Solamnia. The Dragon Army is two days south. The people of Vogler are making dinner.

Whatever comes next, you will be ready.

> **Your Prelude Ends Here**
> Turn to the Order Outcome section for your squiring ceremony results and title. Then turn to the Romance Epilogue section. Then turn to THE BRIDGE TO THE CAMPAIGN.

> **A) The Oath is taken. Vogler is ahead. The Dragon Army is behind. Begin.** --> Scene 71

---


## SCENE 71 — THE LAST NIGHT BEFORE

*Vogler — The Watchtower, Night*

You cannot sleep. Neither can anyone else, but you are the only one who has climbed the watchtower to stand where you swore your oath hours ago and look south at the dark.

The smoke is closer. You can see it against the stars — not a column anymore but a smear, wide and low, the signature of an army that is burning everything in its path and does not care who sees the light. It is closer than it was at sunset. Closer than Caeron estimated. Somewhere in that dark there are two thousand soldiers and they are coming here because a lake village of eighty-four people happens to stand between them and wherever they are going next.

The lake is black glass. Two of the moons are up — Solinari white and steady, Lunitari red at the edge. Their light catches the water and the bridge and the rooftops below you and the single candle still burning in Isak's house where Caeron is bent over the map, still working, still trying to make the arithmetic come out differently. It will not. He knows. He works anyway.

You have been many things in the weeks since Brightholm. A recruit who did not know what a tabard weighed. A student of the Measure who argued with Aldous about the difference between law and justice. A fighter or a paladin — someone who learned to hold a blade and mean it. A squire who walked the south road and saw what the Dragon Army leaves behind and did not look away. A person who made choices that mattered to people who were watching you make them.

You think about the choices. Not the large ones — not the battles, not the confrontations, not the moments that would end up in Thessaly's chronicle. The small ones. The moment you chose to sit with Mira instead of walking past. The conversation where Aldric stopped being a rival and became something else. The decision to stay at the field exercise when leaving was an option. The people on the south road — the refugees, the wounded, the merchant with the broken cart — and what you did when you reached them. Every choice a thread. Every thread part of the weave that brought you here, standing on old stone, looking at smoke, wearing an oath you mean.

**IF YOU HAVE AN ACTIVE ROMANCE:** You think about them. Not in the way of poetry or grand gestures — in the way of someone who has found another person in the middle of a catastrophe and recognised them. The way their voice sounds when they are tired. The expression they make when they think no one is watching. The specific weight of their presence beside you, which has become so familiar that you cannot remember what the space felt like before they filled it. Whatever happens tomorrow, whatever the smoke brings, you are grateful for the finding.

**IF YOUR CORRUPTION TRACK IS QUESTIONING OR HIGHER:** You think about the other thing, too. The doubt. The moments where the Oath felt like a cage instead of a compass. The conversations that left cracks in what you believed. Standing here, those cracks have not healed. They are part of you now — part of the knight you are becoming, which is not the knight the training manuals describe but is perhaps, in this broken age on this broken world, the knight that is needed. You do not resolve the doubt tonight. You carry it. It is heavier than the sword and more important.

Below you, Vogler is asleep. Not all of it — Bess is up, baking bread for the morning, because bread is what she knows and what she knows is what she can give. Torren is on the shore, checking the boats one more time. Somewhere a dog is barking at shadows. The children are asleep. Tam is asleep. He will wake up tomorrow in a world where knights are still invincible because you and Aldric and Caeron and everyone who climbed this ridge are going to make sure that he does.

You stand on the watchtower for a long time. The wind comes off the lake. It smells of water and pine and, very faintly, of smoke.

Then you go down. There is work to do in the morning, and you intend to be rested for it.

*The Prelude to The Knight's Oath ends here. The campaign begins at dawn.*

> **Your Prelude is Complete.**
> Turn to the **Order Outcome** section for your squiring ceremony results and title.
> Then turn to the **Romance Epilogue** section for your companion's oath-ending scene.
> Then turn to **THE BRIDGE TO THE CAMPAIGN** for what you carry forward.
>
> *Est Sularus oth Mithas.*

---





---


# ORDER OUTCOMES — SQUIRE OF YOUR ORDER

---


## SQUIRE OF THE ROSE

**Motto:** Est Sularus oth Mithas — My honour is my life.

You earned this not through outstanding sword work but through asking the right questions and standing by the answers. The Rose values wisdom and moral courage. Ser Aldous was present at your oath. He did not try to hide that he was moved.

> **Squire Title Granted**
> Squire-Scholar of the Rose. Addressed as 'Squire', given deference in matters of law and the Measure.

> **Fighter Ability (carry into main campaign)**
> Historical Insight (1/day): Ask the DM one true lore question. Advantage on Persuasion checks when appealing to someone's sense of justice.

> **Paladin Ability (carry into main campaign)**
> Voice of Mercy (1/day): When you use Lay on Hands on a creature, they are also freed of one condition (frightened, charmed, or poisoned). Advantage on Persuasion with any NPC who has seen you act against personal interest to protect someone.

**Your Road to Vogler:**
You ride into Vogler carrying the weight of what justice means when law fails it. Start with +2 Persuasion and the trait: Voice of the Rose (NPCs who see you protect someone at personal cost trust you immediately).

---


## SQUIRE OF THE SWORD

**Motto:** The blade that does not waver.

You earned this on the south road, on the ridge, at the bridge — in every fight where you chose to stand rather than step back. Aldric was in the front row at your oath. You saw him.

> **Squire Title Granted**
> Squire-Warrior of the Sword. Addressed as 'Squire', first position in any fighting formation.

> **Fighter Ability (carry into main campaign)**
> Combat Resolve (1/day): When reduced to 0 HP, make one final attack before falling. When you succeed on a death save, use Second Wind as a reaction.

> **Paladin Ability (carry into main campaign)**
> Righteous Fury (1/day): When you use Divine Smite, you may choose to double the radiant damage. The first time in each combat that an ally is reduced to 0 HP within 30 ft. of you, your next attack before the end of your next turn has Advantage.

**Your Road to Vogler:**
You ride into Vogler knowing what is coming will need everything you have. Start with +2 Athletics and the trait: Blade-Oath (advantage on first attack of any combat).

---


## SQUIRE OF THE CROWN

**Motto:** Law is the shield of the innocent.

You kept the law when others would have broken it and understood that duty is the structure that lets conscience act in the world. Lord Caeron conducted your squiring ceremony himself. Afterwards, in private, he shook your hand instead of saluting. That is not in the protocol.

> **Squire Title Granted**
> Squire-Commander of the Crown (junior). Given deference in command decisions.

> **Fighter Ability (carry into main campaign)**
> Authority of the Crown (1/day): Issue a command to a non-hostile NPC. They comply unless it directly harms them. Advantage on Intimidation checks against Dragon Army soldiers.

> **Paladin Ability (carry into main campaign)**
> Aura of Command (passive): Your Aura of Protection (CHA +2 to saves) also applies to allies' attack rolls against enemies that are frightened, stunned, or prone. Authority of the Crown (1/day): same as Fighter version.

**Your Road to Vogler:**
You ride into Vogler as the Order's standard. People will look to you when things fall apart. Start with +2 Persuasion/Intimidation and the trait: Command Presence (NPCs do not panic in your presence during combat).


# ROMANCE EPILOGUES — OATH ENDINGS


## SCENE 72 — ALDRIC VOSS: THE THING HE KEPT

*Vogler — Watchtower Steps, After the Oath*

Everyone else has gone down. The ceremony is over, the sunset is a memory, and Vogler's cook fires are the only light left except for the moons and the distant glow of what you both know is not a sunset in the south. Aldric is sitting on the watchtower steps with his back against Solamnic stone, and he has not moved, and you understand that he was waiting for everyone to leave because what he has to say is not for an audience.

You sit beside him. The stone is cold. His shoulder touches yours and neither of you moves away.

'I have been carrying something,' he says. 'Since Brightholm.' He reaches into the inside pocket of his tabard and pulls out a piece of paper, folded so many times the creases have become soft. He unfolds it. In the moonlight you can see his handwriting — the careful, slightly too-formal script of a man who was raised to believe penmanship reflects character.

It is a list. Your name is at the top. Below it, in a column, dates. Beside each date, a note:

*Day 3 — Smiled at me during formation. Probably meant nothing.*
*Day 11 — Sparring. Their footwork is better than mine. Furious about it. Also something else.*
*Day 19 — They sat with me at evening mess when no one else did. I do not know what to do with this.*
*Day 34 — I know what to do with this.*

There are more. Weeks of them. A record of falling kept in the precise format of a military log because Aldric Voss does not know any other way to process what is happening to him except to document it and hope the data resolves into something he can act on.

'I spent eight months being competitive with you,' he says. 'I think that was because I did not know what else to do with it.' He folds the paper. Unfolds it. Folds it again. His hands are doing the thing they do when he is nervous — the same hands that held a sword on the south road without shaking. 'I know now.'

He does not say *I love you*. Aldric Voss does not have those words yet — they are too large, too unstructured, too much like jumping without knowing where the ground is. What he does is put the paper in your hand. The list. The entire record of his falling. He gives it to you the way he gives everything: completely, precisely, with the understanding that once given it cannot be taken back.

'That is yours,' he says. 'It was always yours. I was just holding it.'

You sit on the watchtower steps and the moons move and the smoke in the south grows closer and Aldric's shoulder is warm against yours and the paper in your hand is the weight of eight months of a man learning to stop competing and start choosing.

He does not ask what comes next. He already knows. He will be at the wall. He will be wherever you are. He will be the person who shows up, every time, not because the Oath requires it but because Aldric Voss decided, on a day he noted in his careful handwriting, that showing up is what his hands are for.

*In the main campaign, Aldric Voss is a named companion with a personal quest line. His loyalty is absolute. His competitive streak remains — but now it is pointed outward, at the enemy, at the world, at anything that threatens the person whose name is at the top of a much-folded piece of paper.*


## SCENE 73 — THESSALY BRIGHTWATER: THE THIRD COLUMN

*Vogler — The Watchtower Wall, Night*

She finds you an hour after the Oath. She is carrying her notebook — the one she has carried since Brightholm, the one with the leather cover that has been rained on and slept on and used as a writing surface in conditions that would make a professional archivist weep. But she is also carrying something else: a second notebook. New. The spine uncracked.

'I need to interview you one more time,' she says. She sits on the wall beside you, feet dangling over the village. Below, Vogler is quiet. 'For the record. The chronicle. The official account of the Knight's Oath prelude.' She opens the old notebook. Pages of her small, precise handwriting — every scene, every conversation, every choice, reduced to ink and observation. 'What do you want people to remember about what happened here?'

You tell her. She writes it down with the focused attention she gives everything — the pen moving steadily, her eyes flicking between you and the page, her lower lip caught between her teeth the way it gets when she is composing in real time.

Then she stops writing.

She closes the notebook. Sets it on the wall between you. Looks at you without the professional distance, without the journalist's careful frame, without the layer of observation that has been between her and everything since you met her.

'I stopped being a journalist about you a while ago,' she says. 'I have been telling myself otherwise. I have three columns in this notebook — facts, analysis, and personal. The personal column is longer than the other two combined. My editor would have my credential for that.' She pauses. 'I do not care about my credential.'

She picks up the new notebook. Opens it to the first page. It is blank. She writes one line at the top, and when she turns it toward you, you read: *What comes next. — T.B.*

'The first notebook is the record,' she says. 'This one is not. This one is for whatever we write after the record is closed.' She looks at you with the expression of a woman who has spent her professional life watching other people's stories and has just decided, consciously and irreversibly, to step into one. 'I would like there to be a *we* in the next volume. If that is something you want.'

Below you, Vogler. Above you, the moons. Beside you, a woman who has put down the only armour she has ever worn — her notebook, her distance, her professional observer's detachment — and is sitting beside you unarmed.

The new notebook is between you. The first page has one line. The rest is blank.

There is so much room.

*In the main campaign, Thessaly Brightwater provides intelligence, political context, and the perspective of someone who sees clearly because seeing clearly is what she does. She covers for you in the press, steers public opinion, and writes the chronicle that will define how history remembers what happened at Vogler. She loves you with attention — the most valuable thing she has, and she does not give it to anything that does not matter.*


## SCENE 74 — COMMANDER SERATH: WHAT DEFECTION COSTS

*Vogler — The Lake Shore, Before Dawn*

They find you in the hour before dawn when the sky is grey and the lake is the colour of steel and even the birds have not started yet. Serath moves quietly — they have always moved quietly, and you have learned not to startle when they appear at the edge of your awareness like a thought you did not know you were having.

'I do not sleep before battles,' they say. 'A habit from the other side. The Dragon Army does not encourage rest the night before an engagement. They encourage — preparedness.' The word costs them something. You can see it in the set of their jaw, the way they look at the lake instead of at you.

They sit beside you on the dock. Their boots hang over the water. In the grey light, the scar on their neck — the one they never talk about, the one that runs from ear to collarbone in a line too clean to be combat — is visible. You have wondered about it. You have not asked. Some questions are invitations and some are intrusions, and you have learned the difference.

'I am going to tell you something,' Serath says. 'Not because you need to know it for the battle. Because you need to know it for — the other thing. The thing between us.'

They pull off one glove. Their hand in the grey light is mapped with scars — not combat scars, not the marks of a soldier's life. Burns. Small, precise, deliberate. The kind of marks that are made by someone who wanted to remind themselves what pain felt like so they could remain familiar with it as a tool.

'Dragon Army conditioning,' they say. 'For officers. You learn to administer pain before you learn to administer orders. They do not call it torture. They call it *calibration.*' They put the glove back on. 'I have calibrated people. I have calibrated myself. I have done things that your Oath would not survive knowing about.'

They look at you. Their eyes in the pre-dawn are dark and complicated and absolutely honest. 'I am trying to become the kind of person who deserves what you are offering. I have not succeeded yet. I may not. The arithmetic of what I have done and what I am doing does not balance. It may never balance.'

A silence. The lake. The grey sky. The smell of water.

'But I am trying. I have not been trying for anyone before. I did not think trying was a thing I was capable of. I thought I was — finished. Calibrated. Final.' They look at their gloved hands. 'I am not final. I do not know what I am. I know that I am here, and I did not have to be, and I chose to be, and the choosing was the hardest thing I have done since defecting.'

They stand. They offer you their hand — the gloved one, the scarred one, the hand that has done terrible things and is learning, in its own way, to do other things.

'Whatever happens tomorrow,' they say. 'I will be at the wall. Not because of your Oath. Because of mine. The one I am making now. To you.'

The dawn comes. Serath does not let go of your hand until it does.

*In the main campaign, Commander Serath is a morally complex ally whose intelligence on the Dragon Army is invaluable and whose past is a minefield. They are loyal to you in a way that transcends institution — and that loyalty creates political complications that will define your journey through Vogler and beyond. They love you the way a person loves who has been taught that love is a vulnerability: fiercely, warily, and with the constant knowledge that what they feel is more dangerous than anything the Dragon Army ever taught them.*


## SCENE 75 — MIRA OF THE OAKS: WHAT THE HEALER KEEPS

*Vogler — The Field Hospital, Night*

You find her in the grain store — the one Isak gave her for the field hospital. She has been working since the planning session ended: clearing space, laying out bandages, hanging herbs from the rafters to dry, organising her supplies in the specific order she uses — the order that means she can find a tourniquet in the dark, by touch, in under three seconds. You have watched her work before. You have never watched her work for a battle she knows she will lose.

She does not look up when you come in. Her hands are sorting yarrow — the dried stems snap with a sound like small bones. 'There is not enough,' she says. 'There is never enough, but this is — specifically not enough. I can treat minor wounds for twenty people or critical wounds for four. If the Dragon Army breaches the line, the number of critical wounds will not be four.'

She puts the yarrow down. Her hands are shaking. You have seen Mira's hands steady over gut wounds, burns, fever, the compound fracture on the south road that would have made a veteran surgeon flinch. Her hands do not shake. Her hands are shaking now.

'I was not looking for this,' she says.

She does not mean the battle.

She picks up a bandage. Puts it down. Picks it up. Her hands want to work because working is what they know and what they know is all she has to give, and tonight, in this grain store that smells of wheat and dried fish and the faint sweetness of yarrow, it is not enough, and she knows it, and the knowing is breaking something in her that she has kept very carefully unbroken since her mother's village.

'My mother was a healer,' she says. You know this. She has told you, once, in the way people tell things that matter — quickly, without detail, in a sentence that contains an entire life. 'She had a garden. Yarrow, comfrey, marigold, the things that grow when you ask them to. She taught me to tie a bandage when I was six. I had good hands, she said. Steady.' Mira looks at her hands. They are still shaking. 'The village burned. She stayed to treat the wounded. I left because she told me to leave. She was — she was a better healer than I am. She was a better person than I am. She stayed.'

The silence in the grain store is the kind that holds everything.

'I keep trying to be her,' Mira says. 'Every wound I dress, every fever I break, every time I reach for the yarrow, I am trying to be my mother. And I cannot be. Because she stayed and I left and the village burned and she —'

She stops. The yarrow is in her lap. Her hands are still.

You sit beside her. You do not touch her, because Mira has always been precise about touch — when it is welcome, when it is work, when it is both. You sit and you wait and the silence is the kind that does not need filling because filling it would be a disservice to what is being trusted to you.

'I was not looking for this,' she says again. 'You. This. Whatever this is that makes me shake when I have spent years learning not to. I do not have a framework for it. I have yarrow and bandages and good hands and a mother who would have known what to say and I do not know what to say so I am going to say the only thing I know.'

She takes your hand. Her grip is precise. The grip of a healer: firm, warm, the pressure exactly where it should be.

'I will be in the grain store,' she says. 'Tomorrow. And every day after that. Not because of an oath. Because my hands are steady when you are in the room and I do not know why and I have decided to stop asking why and just — be grateful. For the steadiness. For the room. For you.'

Her hands are not shaking anymore.

*In the main campaign, Mira of the Oaks is the party's healer and the person you fight hardest to keep standing — not because she is fragile, but because she is the one who keeps everyone else standing and someone needs to keep her standing too. She loves you with competence, with presence, with the steady hands of a woman who has learned that the best way to say I love you is to be there when the bleeding starts, every time, without being asked.*


## SCENE 76 — LORD CAERON ASHVALE: THE REPORT

*Vogler — Elder Isak's House, Late Night*

He is still at the map when you find him. Of course he is. The candle has burned to a stub — the whale oil lamp ran dry an hour ago and he replaced it with a tallow candle that gives worse light and he has not complained because Lord Caeron Ashvale does not waste time on complaints when there is work to do.

You stand in the doorway. He does not look up. His pen is moving — not on the map anymore, on a sheet of paper. His handwriting, which is normally precise to the point of being mechanical, has loosened. He is tired. He is more tired than you have ever seen him, and you have seen him lead a forced march for three days and deliver a briefing at the end of it that sounded like he had slept eight hours.

'Your evaluation report,' he says. He does not look up. 'I need to complete it before tomorrow. Regulations require that squire evaluations be filed before the assigning knight enters combat.' He writes another line. 'The regulations do not anticipate that the combat will be against a force that outnumbers us twenty to one. But regulations are regulations.'

He finishes. Signs it. Sets the pen down with the care of a man who has just completed something that matters more than the paper it is written on. Then he looks up at you, and his face does the thing — the thing where the composure is there but the thing behind it is visible, the way a lamp is visible through a curtain.

'Come here,' he says. 'I want you to read it.'

You cross the room. The evaluation form is standard Solamnic — you have seen blank ones in Aldous's office. Sections for combat proficiency, adherence to the Measure, leadership potential, character assessment. Caeron has filled them all in his loosened, tired handwriting. The marks are high. Higher than you expected.

In the Overall Assessment box — the one that determines whether a squire advances, the one that goes to the Chapter Council, the one that matters — he has written:

*This squire will be remarkable. I have no further notes.*

You look at him. He looks at you. The composure is not gone — Caeron's composure is structural, it is load-bearing, it will be the last thing to go — but it is thinner than you have ever seen it, and what is behind it is not the commander you have followed since Brightholm. It is the man. Just the man. Tired and afraid and steady and looking at you with an expression that has taken him weeks to assemble from the parts available to a person who was not built for vulnerability.

'The Order of the Crown values constancy,' he says. His voice is quiet. The commander's voice is a projection; this one is not. This is the voice that exists in the space between the rank and the person. 'Constancy in duty. Constancy in service. Constancy in —' He stops. Starts again. 'I intend to be constant. In all the ways that word means. If you will allow it.'

He stands. He is taller than you in the lamplight. He picks up the evaluation and folds it and puts it in your hand, and his fingers stay on yours for a moment longer than regulations require.

'You should sleep,' he says. 'Tomorrow will be — tomorrow.'

He does not move toward the door. Neither do you. The candle burns. The map is on the table. The evaluation is in your hand. Lord Caeron Ashvale is standing very close to you and the thing behind his composure is fully visible now and it is not command and it is not duty and it is not constancy.

It is need. Simple, human, completely ungovernable need. The kind that no regulation covers, because whoever wrote the regulations never stood in a room with a single candle the night before a battle and felt the distance between two people become something that could not be maintained.

'To hell with the command structure,' he says. 'Just for tonight.'

He does not finish the sentence. He does not need to.

*In the main campaign, Lord Caeron Ashvale provides strategic resources, command authority, and a quest line about what he has been hiding from the Order since before Brightholm. He loves you the way he commands: with absolute commitment, careful planning, and the private knowledge that no plan survives contact with the person he is in love with. The command structure he abandoned for one night becomes, quietly and permanently, the structure he rebuilds around both of you.*


## SCENE 77 — SERA NIGHTHOLLOW: THE PERIMETER

*Vogler — Eastern Ridge, Before Dawn*

She is already awake. Of course she is. You find her on the eastern ridge, crouched at the tree line, bow unstrung, watching the southern dark with the patience of a person who has spent years learning that the most dangerous things announce themselves through absence — the bird that stops singing, the branch that does not move, the silence that means something is about to stop being silent.

'You should be sleeping,' she says. She does not look at you. Her eyes are on the tree line.

'So should you.'

'I do not sleep before operations.' A pause. 'I do not sleep well in general. I have been told this is a personality flaw.' Another pause, shorter. 'Among other things.'

You sit beside her. The grass is cold with dew. The ridge gives a view of the southern approach — the road, the tree line, the distant smear of smoke that is closer than it was at sunset. In the moonlight, Sera's profile is sharp and still, the face of someone who has trained herself to be a weapon and is only now, slowly, learning that weapons can also be held.

'I should tell you something,' she says. She picks up a twig. Breaks it. Precise, clean, the snap of someone who does everything with the minimum necessary force. 'I am not easy to be with. I have been told this. By people who tried and decided the effort was not proportional to the return. I am — clinical. I observe rather than feel. I assess threat levels in rooms that do not contain threats. I catalogued your physical capabilities before I learned your name. When you smile I do not see the smile first. I see the musculature. The dental alignment. The—' She stops. Breaks another twig. 'This is what I mean.'

You tell her you are not particularly easy either.

She looks at you. In the moonlight, the thing behind her assessment — behind the clinical distance, behind the catalogued capabilities and the threat levels and the musculature — is visible. It is not warmth, exactly. Sera does not do warmth the way other people do warmth. It is something harder and more precise: it is the decision to be present with someone, fully and without reservation, having assessed every risk and found them acceptable.

'All right,' she says. And then, not smiling but with the thing behind her eyes that is equivalent: 'All right.'

She goes back to watching the tree line. After a moment, she shifts — just slightly, just enough that her shoulder touches yours. It is the smallest gesture she is capable of and it contains everything: the scout who stays, the ranger who could vanish into any forest in Ansalon and has chosen this ridge, this moment, this person.

'I will be at the eastern approach tomorrow,' she says. 'If the scouts come through the trees, I will see them. If they come through the shallows, I will hear them. If they get past me—' She pauses. 'They will not get past me.'

It is not a declaration of love. It is better. It is a declaration of competence, offered to the person she has decided is worth being competent for.

The dawn comes slowly. You watch it together, shoulder to shoulder, in a silence that is not empty but full — full of the things Sera does not say because she does not have the words, and the things you do not say because you do not need to, and the tree line that holds its breath, and the ridge that holds you both.

*In the main campaign, Sera Nighthollow is a scout companion with her own quest line. Her loyalty is expressed through capability — she keeps you safe not with words but with the silent, tireless, precise work of a woman who has decided that your survival is her personal responsibility. She loves you the way she watches a perimeter: completely, without rest, and with the understanding that the moment she stops paying attention is the moment everything falls apart.*


## SCENE 78 — VAEL ASHMORE: NO REGRETS

*Vogler — The Training Ground, Dawn*

She is running sword forms. Of course she is. The sun is not fully up and Vael Ashmore is in the cleared space behind the watchtower, moving through the forms with the precise, fierce energy of someone who processes emotion through her body because her body is the instrument she trusts most.

You watch her for a moment from the watchtower steps. The forms are Solamnic standard — you recognise them, you have practiced them together — but there is something different in how she is moving this morning. Faster. Harder. The blade cutting the air with a sound like tearing cloth. She is not practicing. She is deciding something.

She sees you. She does not stop. She finishes the form — a twelve-count sequence that ends with the blade extended, the weight forward, the body committed to the strike in a way that leaves nothing in reserve. Then she lowers the sword and looks at you, breathing hard, her face flushed with exertion and something else.

'Good,' she says. 'I was going to come find you. I have something to say and I would rather say it now than say it at the wall when there is not time to mean it.'

She sheathes the sword. Walks to you. She moves the way she always moves — direct, no wasted motion, the stride of a woman who knows where she is going even when the destination is unprecedented.

'I do not do the thing where people are strange in the morning after something real,' she says. 'I have seen people do it. The awkwardness. The renegotiation. The pretending it was less than it was.' She is standing very close. The sweat on her throat catches the first light. 'I do not pretend. It is the one luxury I allow myself.'

She hands you your gear — she collected it from the watchtower, your belt and blade and tabard, and she has cleaned the tabard. You did not ask her to. She did it because it needed doing and because Vael Ashmore's love language is acts of service performed without acknowledgment.

'For what it is worth,' she says. 'I have no regrets.'

Then you are both laughing. You do not know which of you started — it breaks out of the morning like a surprise, this laughter on the edge of a battle, on the edge of everything, and Vael's laugh is the one she does not perform for anyone, the real one, the one that shakes her shoulders and reaches her eyes and is, for a moment, louder than the fear.

'I want to do this again,' she says. The laughing has stopped. Her eyes are bright and fierce and completely honest. 'Not the battle. The rest of it. The — the thing where I come back from the forms and you are here. The morning. The gear. The—' She gestures between you. 'This. Whatever this is that makes me run the forms faster because I have something to come back to.'

She looks south. The smoke.

'After,' she says. And it is not a wish. It is a plan. Vael Ashmore does not wish. She decides, and then she makes the decision real with her hands and her sword and her absolute refusal to regret anything she has chosen.

'After. When there is an after. I will be here. Running forms. Waiting for you to show up with that expression on your face.' She grins. It is the grin of a woman going to war with someone she has chosen, and the choosing has made her dangerous in a way that has nothing to do with the sword. 'Do not be late.'

*In the main campaign, Vael Ashmore is a Crown-path companion with her own quest line. She fights beside you with the specific ferocity of a woman who has found something worth protecting and intends to protect it with every tool available to her, including her sword, her stubbornness, and her completely ungovernable refusal to let the world take anything else from her. She loves you the way she fights: all in, no retreat, no regrets.*


## SCENE 79 — TARIEL DUSKMANTLE: THE OBSERVER OBSERVED

*Vogler — The Watchtower Wall, Night*

She is sitting on the wall with her notebook closed. You have never seen her notebook closed. In every memory you have of Tariel Duskmantle — every conversation, every briefing, every meal, every moment of stillness between moments of crisis — the notebook has been open, the pen has been moving, the observation has been ongoing. The notebook closed is Tariel disarmed. Tariel without her frame. Tariel as she is when she is not constructing the version of herself that interfaces with the world.

You sit beside her. The wall is cold. Below, Vogler is quiet.

'I did not take the Oath,' she says. 'I watched you take it. I wrote one line.' She taps the closed notebook. 'I will not tell you what it was. Not yet. Possibly not ever, depending on how the next several days proceed. It is — personal. I do not do personal well. I do personal the way other people do surgery: carefully, with tools, from behind a sterile barrier.'

She looks at you. Without the notebook, without the pen, without the journalist's armour of observation and analysis, Tariel's face is different. Younger. Less composed.
